<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Antibiotic</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Antimicrobial substance active against bacteria</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Antibacterial" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Antibacterial_(disambiguation)" title="Antibacterial (disambiguation)">Antibacterial (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about treatment of bacterial infection. For anti-tumor antibiotics, see <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy#Cytotoxic_antibiotics" title="Chemotherapy">Chemotherapy § Cytotoxic antibiotics</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="background-color: #ddbbee">Antibiotic</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-subheader" colspan="2"><i><a href="/wiki/Drug_class" title="Drug class">Drug class</a></i></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Staphylococcus_aureus_(AB_Test).jpg"><img alt="Staphylococcus aureus (AB Test).jpg" data-file-height="2097" data-file-width="2315" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Staphylococcus_aureus_%28AB_Test%29.jpg/280px-Staphylococcus_aureus_%28AB_Test%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Staphylococcus_aureus_%28AB_Test%29.jpg/420px-Staphylococcus_aureus_%28AB_Test%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Staphylococcus_aureus_%28AB_Test%29.jpg/560px-Staphylococcus_aureus_%28AB_Test%29.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a><div class="infobox-caption">Testing the susceptibility of <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> to antibiotics by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirby-Bauer_antibiotic_testing" title="Kirby-Bauer antibiotic testing">Kirby-Bauer disk diffusion method</a> – antibiotics diffuse from antibiotic-containing disks and inhibit growth of <i>S. aureus</i>, resulting in a zone of inhibition.</div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="background: #e8e8e8; text-align: center"><a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q12187" title="d:Q12187">In Wikidata</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <b>antibiotic</b> is a type of <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> substance active against <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>. It is the most important type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibacterial_agent" title="Antibacterial agent">antibacterial agent</a> for fighting <a href="/wiki/Pathogenic_bacteria" title="Pathogenic bacteria">bacterial infections</a>, and antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medications</a> are widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Therapy" title="Therapy">treatment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_prophylaxis" title="Antibiotic prophylaxis">prevention</a> of such infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHSB_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHSB-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> They may either <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">kill</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bacteriostatic_agent" title="Bacteriostatic agent">inhibit the growth</a> of bacteria. A limited number of antibiotics also possess <a href="/wiki/Antiprotozoal" title="Antiprotozoal">antiprotozoal</a> activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibioticandantiprotozal_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antibioticandantiprotozal-4">[4]</a></sup> Antibiotics are not effective against <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a> or <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> drugs which inhibit viruses are termed <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">antiviral drugs</a> or antivirals rather than antibiotics.
</p><p>Sometimes, the term <i>antibiotic</i>—literally "opposing life", from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> roots ἀντι <i>anti</i>, "against" and βίος <i>bios</i>, "life"—is broadly used to refer to any substance used against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a>, but in the usual medical usage, antibiotics (such as <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>) are those produced naturally (by one <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganism</a> fighting another), whereas non-antibiotic antibacterials (such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide" title="Sulfonamide">sulfonamides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptics</a>) are <a href="/wiki/Total_synthesis" title="Total synthesis">fully synthetic</a>. However, both classes have the same goal of killing or preventing the growth of microorganisms, and both are included in <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_chemotherapy" title="Antimicrobial chemotherapy">antimicrobial chemotherapy</a>. "Antibacterials" include <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> drugs, <a href="/wiki/Antibacterial_soap" title="Antibacterial soap">antibacterial soaps</a>, and chemical <a href="/wiki/Disinfectant" title="Disinfectant">disinfectants</a>, whereas antibiotics are an important class of antibacterials used more specifically in medicine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_use_in_livestock" title="Antibiotic use in livestock">sometimes in livestock feed</a>.
</p><p>Antibiotics have been used since ancient times. Many civilizations used topical application of moldy bread, with many references to its beneficial effects arising from ancient Egypt, Nubia, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, Greece, and Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The first person to directly document the use of molds to treat infections was <a href="/wiki/John_Parkinson_(botanist)" title="John Parkinson (botanist)">John Parkinson</a> (1567–1650). Antibiotics revolutionized medicine in the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Fleming" title="Alexander Fleming">Alexander Fleming</a> (1881–1955) discovered modern day <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a> in 1928, the widespread use of which proved significantly beneficial during wartime. However, the effectiveness and easy access to antibiotics have also led to their <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_misuse" title="Antibiotic misuse">overuse</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and some bacteria have evolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">resistance</a> to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHSB_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHSB-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> has classified <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antimicrobial resistance</a> as a widespread "serious threat [that] is no longer a prediction for the future, it is happening right now in every region of the world and has the potential to affect anyone, of any age, in any country".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-12">[12]</a></sup> Global deaths attributable to antimicrobial resistance numbered 1.27 million in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term 'antibiosis', meaning "against life", was introduced by the French bacteriologist <a href="/wiki/Jean_Paul_Vuillemin" title="Jean Paul Vuillemin">Jean Paul Vuillemin</a> as a descriptive name of the phenomenon exhibited by these early antibacterial drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CALDERIN2007_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-CALDERIN2007-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saxena_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saxena-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Antibiosis was first described in 1877 in bacteria when <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> observed that an airborne bacillus could inhibit the growth of <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saxena_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saxena-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> These drugs were later renamed antibiotics by <a href="/wiki/Selman_Waksman" title="Selman Waksman">Selman Waksman</a>, an American microbiologist, in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>antibiotic</i> was first used in 1942 by <a href="/wiki/Selman_Waksman" title="Selman Waksman">Selman Waksman</a> and his collaborators in journal articles to describe any substance produced by a microorganism that is <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/antagonism" title="wikt:antagonism">antagonistic</a> to the growth of other microorganisms in high dilution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saxena_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Saxena-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakeman1947_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wakeman1947-19">[19]</a></sup> This definition excluded substances that kill bacteria but that are not produced by microorganisms (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastric_juices" title="Gastric juices">gastric juices</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>). It also excluded <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthetic</a> antibacterial compounds such as the <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(medicine)" title="Sulfonamide (medicine)">sulfonamides</a>. In current usage, the term "antibiotic" is applied to any medication that kills bacteria or inhibits their growth, regardless of whether that medication is produced by a microorganism or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "antibiotic" derives from <i>anti</i> + βιωτικός (<i>biōtikos</i>), "fit for life, lively",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> which comes from βίωσις (<i>biōsis</i>), "way of life",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and that from βίος (<i>bios</i>), "life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibiotics_FAQ_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antibiotics_FAQ-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The term "antibacterial" derives from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> ἀντί (<i>anti</i>), "against"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> + βακτήριον (<i>baktērion</i>), diminutive of βακτηρία (<i>baktēria</i>), "staff, cane",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> because the first bacteria to be discovered were rod.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">Usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_uses">Medical uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medical uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Antibiotics are used to treat or prevent bacterial infections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibiotic_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antibiotic-28">[28]</a></sup> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Protozoan_infection" title="Protozoan infection">protozoan infections</a>. (<a href="/wiki/Metronidazole" title="Metronidazole">Metronidazole</a> is effective against a number of <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_disease" title="Parasitic disease">parasitic diseases</a>). When an infection is suspected of being responsible for an illness but the responsible pathogen has not been identified, an <a href="/wiki/Empiric_therapy" title="Empiric therapy">empiric therapy</a> is adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-General_principles_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-General_principles-29">[29]</a></sup> This involves the administration of a <a href="/wiki/Broad-spectrum_antibiotic" title="Broad-spectrum antibiotic">broad-spectrum antibiotic</a> based on the signs and symptoms presented and is initiated pending laboratory results that can take several days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibiotic_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Antibiotic-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-General_principles_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-General_principles-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the responsible pathogenic microorganism is already known or has been identified, <a href="/wiki/Therapy#Definitive_therapy" title="Therapy">definitive therapy</a> can be started. This will usually involve the use of a narrow-spectrum antibiotic. The choice of antibiotic given will also be based on its cost. Identification is critically important as it can reduce the cost and toxicity of the antibiotic therapy and also reduce the possibility of the emergence of antimicrobial resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-General_principles_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-General_principles-29">[29]</a></sup> To avoid surgery, antibiotics may be given for non-complicated acute <a href="/wiki/Appendicitis" title="Appendicitis">appendicitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antibiotics may be given as a <a href="/wiki/Preventive_healthcare" title="Preventive healthcare">preventive measure</a> and this is usually limited to at-risk populations such as those with a <a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">weakened immune system</a> (particularly in <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> cases to prevent <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>), those taking <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppressive_drug" title="Immunosuppressive drug">immunosuppressive drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> patients, and those having <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibiotic_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Antibiotic-28">[28]</a></sup> Their use in surgical procedures is to help prevent infection of <a href="/wiki/Surgical_incision" title="Surgical incision">incisions</a>. They have an important role in <a href="/wiki/Dental_antibiotic_prophylaxis" title="Dental antibiotic prophylaxis">dental antibiotic prophylaxis</a> where their use may prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteremia" title="Bacteremia">bacteremia</a> and consequent <a href="/wiki/Infective_endocarditis" title="Infective endocarditis">infective endocarditis</a>. Antibiotics are also used to prevent infection in cases of <a href="/wiki/Neutropenia" title="Neutropenia">neutropenia</a> particularly cancer-related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of antibiotics for secondary prevention of coronary heart disease is not supported by current scientific evidence, and may actually increase cardiovascular mortality, all-cause mortality and the occurrence of stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Routes_of_administration">Routes of administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Routes of administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Routes_of_administration" title="Routes of administration">routes of administration</a> for antibiotic treatment. Antibiotics are usually <a href="/wiki/Oral_administration" title="Oral administration">taken by mouth</a>. In more severe cases, particularly deep-seated <a href="/wiki/Systemic_disease" title="Systemic disease">systemic infections</a>, antibiotics can be given <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenously</a> or by injection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHSB_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NHSB-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-General_principles_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-General_principles-29">[29]</a></sup> Where the site of infection is easily accessed, antibiotics may be given <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Routes_of_administration#Topical" title="Routes of administration">topically</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Eye_drop" title="Eye drop">eye drops</a> onto the <a href="/wiki/Conjunctiva" title="Conjunctiva">conjunctiva</a> for <a href="/wiki/Conjunctivitis" title="Conjunctivitis">conjunctivitis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ear_drop" title="Ear drop">ear drops</a> for ear infections and acute cases of <a href="/wiki/Otitis_externa" title="Otitis externa">swimmer's ear</a>. Topical use is also one of the treatment options for some skin conditions including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acne_vulgaris" title="Acne vulgaris">acne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellulitis" title="Cellulitis">cellulitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Advantages of topical application include achieving high and sustained concentration of antibiotic at the site of infection; reducing the potential for systemic absorption and toxicity, and total volumes of antibiotic required are reduced, thereby also reducing the risk of antibiotic misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Treating_chronic_wounds_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Treating_chronic_wounds-35">[35]</a></sup> Topical antibiotics applied over certain types of surgical wounds have been reported to reduce the risk of surgical site infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> However, there are certain general causes for concern with topical administration of antibiotics. Some systemic absorption of the antibiotic may occur; the quantity of antibiotic applied is difficult to accurately dose, and there is also the possibility of local <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivity" title="Hypersensitivity">hypersensitivity</a> reactions or <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a> occurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Treating_chronic_wounds_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Treating_chronic_wounds-35">[35]</a></sup> It is recommended to administer antibiotics as soon as possible, especially in life-threatening infections. Many emergency departments stock antibiotics for this purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_consumption">Global consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Global consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Antibiotic consumption varies widely between countries.  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WHO" title="WHO">WHO</a> report on surveillance of antibiotic consumption' published in 2018 analysed 2015 data from 65 countries. As measured in defined daily doses per 1,000 inhabitants per day. Mongolia had the highest consumption with a rate of 64.4. Burundi had the lowest at 4.4.   <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin" title="Amoxicillin">Amoxicillin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin/clavulanic_acid" title="Amoxicillin/clavulanic acid">amoxicillin/clavulanic acid</a> were the most frequently consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Side_effects">Side effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Side effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1275" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf/page1-220px-Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf/page1-330px-Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf/page1-440px-Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Choosing_Wisely_antibiotics_poster_small_English.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Health advocacy messages such as this one encourage patients to talk with their doctor about safety in using antibiotics.</div></div></div>
<p>Antibiotics are screened for any negative effects before their approval for clinical use, and are usually considered safe and well tolerated. However, some antibiotics have been associated with a wide extent of adverse <a href="/wiki/Side_effect" title="Side effect">side effects</a> ranging from mild to very severe depending on the type of antibiotic used, the microbes targeted, and the individual patient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15993671_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15993671-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Side effects may reflect the pharmacological or toxicological properties of the antibiotic or may involve hypersensitivity or <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergic</a> reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibioticandantiprotozal_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Antibioticandantiprotozal-4">[4]</a></sup> Adverse effects range from fever and nausea to major allergic reactions, including <a href="/wiki/Photodermatitis" title="Photodermatitis">photodermatitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common side effects of oral antibiotics include <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, resulting from disruption of the species composition in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestinal_flora" title="Intestinal flora">intestinal flora</a>, resulting, for example, in overgrowth of pathogenic bacteria, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_(bacteria)" title="Clostridium difficile (bacteria)">Clostridium difficile</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Taking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Probiotics" title="Probiotics">probiotics</a> during the course of antibiotic treatment can help prevent antibiotic-associated diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Antibacterials can also affect the <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_flora" title="Vaginal flora">vaginal flora</a>, and may lead to overgrowth of <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> species of the genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candida_(genus)" title="Candida (genus)">Candida</a></i> in the vulvo-vaginal area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pirotta_and_Garland_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pirotta_and_Garland-44">[44]</a></sup> Additional side effects can result from <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">interaction</a> with other drugs, such as the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendon</a> damage from the administration of a <a href="/wiki/Quinolone_antibiotic" title="Quinolone antibiotic">quinolone antibiotic</a> with a systemic <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some antibiotics may also damage the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrion</a>, a bacteria-derived organelle found in eukaryotic, including human, cells.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Mitochondrial damage cause <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a> in cells and has been suggested as a mechanism for side effects from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluoroquinolone" title="Fluoroquinolone">fluoroquinolones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> They are also known to affect <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions">Interactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Interactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_control_pills">Birth control pills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Birth control pills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are few well-controlled studies on whether antibiotic use increases the risk of <a href="/wiki/Oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Oral contraceptive pill">oral contraceptive</a> failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OC_effectiveness_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-OC_effectiveness-48">[48]</a></sup> The majority of studies indicate antibiotics do not interfere with <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">birth control pills</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver1999_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver1999-49">[49]</a></sup> such as clinical studies that suggest the failure rate of contraceptive pills caused by antibiotics is very low (about 1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10384856_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10384856-50">[50]</a></sup> Situations that may increase the risk of oral contraceptive failure include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compliance_(medicine)" title="Compliance (medicine)">non-compliance</a> (missing taking the pill), vomiting, or diarrhea. Gastrointestinal disorders or interpatient variability in oral contraceptive absorption affecting <a href="/wiki/Ethinylestradiol" title="Ethinylestradiol">ethinylestradiol</a> <a href="/wiki/Serum_(blood)" title="Serum (blood)">serum levels</a> in the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OC_effectiveness_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-OC_effectiveness-48">[48]</a></sup> Women with <a href="/wiki/Irregular_menstruation" title="Irregular menstruation">menstrual irregularities</a> may be at higher risk of failure and should be advised to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraception" title="Contraception">backup contraception</a> during antibiotic treatment and for one week after its completion. If patient-specific risk factors for reduced oral contraceptive efficacy are suspected, backup contraception is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OC_effectiveness_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-OC_effectiveness-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In cases where antibiotics have been suggested to affect the efficiency of birth control pills, such as for the broad-spectrum antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Rifampicin" title="Rifampicin">rifampicin</a>, these cases may be due to an increase in the activities of hepatic liver enzymes' causing increased breakdown of the pill's active ingredients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver1999_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver1999-49">[49]</a></sup> Effects on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">intestinal flora</a>, which might result in reduced absorption of <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogens</a> in the colon, have also been suggested, but such suggestions have been inconclusive and controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3155374_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3155374-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2256523_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2256523-52">[52]</a></sup> Clinicians have recommended that extra contraceptive measures be applied during therapies using antibiotics that are suspected to interact with oral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraceptive" title="Contraceptive">contraceptives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver1999_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver1999-49">[49]</a></sup> More studies on the possible interactions between antibiotics and birth control pills (oral contraceptives) are required as well as careful assessment of patient-specific risk factors for potential oral contractive pill failure prior to dismissing the need for backup contraception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OC_effectiveness_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-OC_effectiveness-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol">Alcohol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Alcohol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Interactions between alcohol and certain antibiotics may occur and may cause side effects and decreased effectiveness of antibiotic therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmj_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-bmj-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-antibiotics-and-alcohol_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-antibiotics-and-alcohol-54">[54]</a></sup> While moderate alcohol consumption is unlikely to interfere with many common antibiotics, there are specific types of antibiotics, with which alcohol consumption may cause serious side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHS-55">[55]</a></sup> Therefore, potential risks of side effects and effectiveness depend on the type of antibiotic administered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antibiotics such as <a href="/wiki/Metronidazole" title="Metronidazole">metronidazole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tinidazole" title="Tinidazole">tinidazole</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephamandole" title="Cephamandole">cephamandole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latamoxef" title="Latamoxef">latamoxef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cefoperazone" title="Cefoperazone">cefoperazone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cefmenoxime" title="Cefmenoxime">cefmenoxime</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Furazolidone" title="Furazolidone">furazolidone</a>, cause a <a href="/wiki/Disulfiram" title="Disulfiram">disulfiram</a>-like chemical reaction with alcohol by inhibiting its breakdown by <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde_dehydrogenase" title="Acetaldehyde dehydrogenase">acetaldehyde dehydrogenase</a>, which may result in vomiting, nausea, and shortness of breath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHS-55">[55]</a></sup> In addition, the efficacy of doxycycline and <a href="/wiki/Erythromycin" title="Erythromycin">erythromycin</a> succinate may be reduced by alcohol consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Other effects of alcohol on antibiotic activity include altered activity of the liver enzymes that break down the antibiotic compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antibiotics_FAQ_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Antibiotics_FAQ-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacodynamics">Pharmacodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pharmacodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_pharmacodynamics" title="Antimicrobial pharmacodynamics">Antimicrobial pharmacodynamics</a></div>
<p>The successful outcome of antimicrobial therapy with antibacterial compounds depends on several factors. These include <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">host defense mechanisms</a>, the location of infection, and the pharmacokinetic and pharmacodynamic properties of the antibacterial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pankey2004_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pankey2004-58">[58]</a></sup> The bactericidal activity of antibacterials may depend on the bacterial growth phase, and it often requires ongoing metabolic activity and division of bacterial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bactericidal_action_of_daptomycin_against_stationary-phase_and_nondividing_Staphylococcus_aureus_cells_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bactericidal_action_of_daptomycin_against_stationary-phase_and_nondividing_Staphylococcus_aureus_cells-59">[59]</a></sup> These findings are based on laboratory studies, and in clinical settings have also been shown to eliminate bacterial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pankey2004_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pankey2004-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Since the activity of antibacterials depends frequently on its concentration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhee2004_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhee2004-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>in vitro</i> characterization of antibacterial activity commonly includes the determination of the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_inhibitory_concentration" title="Minimum inhibitory concentration">minimum inhibitory concentration</a> and minimum bactericidal concentration of an antibacterial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pankey2004_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pankey2004-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agar_and_broth_dilution_methods_to_determine_the_minimal_inhibitory_concentration_(MIC)of_antimicrobial_substances_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agar_and_broth_dilution_methods_to_determine_the_minimal_inhibitory_concentration_(MIC)of_antimicrobial_substances-62">[62]</a></sup>
To predict clinical outcome, the antimicrobial activity of an antibacterial is usually combined with its <a href="/wiki/Pharmacokinetics" title="Pharmacokinetics">pharmacokinetic</a> profile, and several pharmacological parameters are used as markers of drug efficacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combination_therapy">Combination therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Combination therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In important infectious diseases, including tuberculosis, <a href="/wiki/Combination_therapy" title="Combination therapy">combination therapy</a> (i.e., the concurrent application of two or more antibiotics) has been used to delay or prevent the emergence of resistance. In acute bacterial infections, antibiotics as part of combination therapy are prescribed for their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_synergy" title="Drug synergy">synergistic</a> effects to improve treatment outcome as the combined effect of both antibiotics is better than their individual effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bollenbach_-_interactions_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bollenbach_-_interactions-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">Methicillin-resistant <i>Staphylococcus aureus</i></a> infections may be treated with a combination therapy of <a href="/wiki/Fusidic_acid" title="Fusidic acid">fusidic acid</a> and rifampicin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic-64">[64]</a></sup> Antibiotics used in combination may also be antagonistic and the combined effects of the two antibiotics may be less than if one of the antibiotics was given as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotherapy" title="Monotherapy">monotherapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic-64">[64]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Chloramphenicol" title="Chloramphenicol">chloramphenicol</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetracyclines" title="Tetracyclines">tetracyclines</a> are antagonists to <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillins</a>. However, this can vary depending on the species of bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In general, combinations of a bacteriostatic antibiotic and bactericidal antibiotic are antagonistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Antagonism_between_bacteriostatic-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bollenbach_-_interactions_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bollenbach_-_interactions-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to combining one antibiotic with another, antibiotics are sometimes co-administered with resistance-modifying agents.  For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-lactam_antibiotics" title="Β-lactam antibiotics">β-lactam antibiotics</a> may be used in combination with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-lactamase_inhibitor" title="Beta-lactamase inhibitor">β-lactamase inhibitors</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Clavulanic_acid" title="Clavulanic acid">clavulanic acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulbactam" title="Sulbactam">sulbactam</a>, when a patient is infected with a <a href="/wiki/Beta-lactamase" title="Beta-lactamase">β-lactamase</a>-producing strain of bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classes">Classes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Classes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_antibiotics" title="List of antibiotics">List of antibiotics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antibiotics_action.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Antibiotics_action.svg/150px-Antibiotics_action.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Antibiotics_action.svg/225px-Antibiotics_action.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Antibiotics_action.svg/300px-Antibiotics_action.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antibiotics_action.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molecular targets of antibiotics on the bacteria cell</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2519" data-file-width="4296" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png/220px-Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png/330px-Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png/440px-Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Protein_synthesis_inhibitors_antibiotic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protein synthesis inhibitors (antibiotics)</div></div></div>
<p>Antibiotics are commonly classified based on their <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_of_action" title="Mechanism of action">mechanism of action</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">chemical structure</a>, or spectrum of activity. Most target bacterial functions or growth processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CALDERIN2007_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-CALDERIN2007-14">[14]</a></sup> Those that target the bacterial cell wall (<a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cephalosporin" title="Cephalosporin">cephalosporins</a>) or the cell membrane (<a href="/wiki/Polymyxin" title="Polymyxin">polymyxins</a>), or interfere with essential bacterial enzymes (<a href="/wiki/Rifamycin" title="Rifamycin">rifamycins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipiarmycin" title="Lipiarmycin">lipiarmycins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quinolone_antibiotic" title="Quinolone antibiotic">quinolones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(medicine)" title="Sulfonamide (medicine)">sulfonamides</a>) have <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">bactericidal</a> activities. <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis_inhibitor" title="Protein synthesis inhibitor">Protein synthesis inhibitors</a> (<a href="/wiki/Macrolide" title="Macrolide">macrolides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lincosamides" title="Lincosamides">lincosamides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tetracycline" title="Tetracycline">tetracyclines</a>) are usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteriostatic" title="Bacteriostatic">bacteriostatic</a> (with the exception of bactericidal <a href="/wiki/Aminoglycoside" title="Aminoglycoside">aminoglycosides</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_importance_of_bactericidal_drugs:_future_directions_in_infectious_disease_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_importance_of_bactericidal_drugs:_future_directions_in_infectious_disease-68">[68]</a></sup> Further categorization is based on their target specificity. "Narrow-spectrum" antibiotics target specific types of bacteria, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-negative" title="Gram-negative">gram-negative</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-positive" title="Gram-positive">gram-positive</a>, whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broad-spectrum_antibiotics" title="Broad-spectrum antibiotics">broad-spectrum antibiotics</a> affect a wide range of bacteria. Following a 40-year break in discovering classes of antibacterial compounds, four new classes of antibiotics were introduced to clinical use in the late 2000s and early 2010s: cyclic <a href="/wiki/Lipopeptide" title="Lipopeptide">lipopeptides</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Daptomycin" title="Daptomycin">daptomycin</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycylcyclines" title="Glycylcyclines">glycylcyclines</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Tigecycline" title="Tigecycline">tigecycline</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxazolidinone" title="Oxazolidinone">oxazolidinones</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Linezolid" title="Linezolid">linezolid</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipiarmycin" title="Lipiarmycin">lipiarmycins</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Fidaxomicin" title="Fidaxomicin">fidaxomicin</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Production_of_antibiotics" title="Production of antibiotics">Production of antibiotics</a></div>
<p>With advances in <a href="/wiki/Medicinal_chemistry" title="Medicinal chemistry">medicinal chemistry</a>, most modern antibacterials are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semisynthetic" title="Semisynthetic">semisynthetic</a> modifications of various natural compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nussbaum2006_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nussbaum2006-71">[71]</a></sup> These include, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Beta-lactam_antibiotics" title="Beta-lactam antibiotics">beta-lactam antibiotics</a>, which include the <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillins</a> (produced by fungi in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i>), the <a href="/wiki/Cephalosporin" title="Cephalosporin">cephalosporins</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Carbapenem" title="Carbapenem">carbapenems</a>. Compounds that are still isolated from living organisms are the <a href="/wiki/Aminoglycoside" title="Aminoglycoside">aminoglycosides</a>, whereas other antibacterials—for example, the <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(medicine)" title="Sulfonamide (medicine)">sulfonamides</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Quinolone_antibiotic" title="Quinolone antibiotic">quinolones</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxazolidinone" title="Oxazolidinone">oxazolidinones</a>—are produced solely by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nussbaum2006_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nussbaum2006-71">[71]</a></sup> Many antibacterial compounds are relatively <a href="/wiki/Small_molecule" title="Small molecule">small molecules</a> with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_weight" title="Molecular weight">molecular weight</a> of less than 1000 <a href="/wiki/Dalton_(unit)" title="Dalton (unit)">daltons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the first pioneering efforts of <a href="/wiki/Howard_Florey" title="Howard Florey">Howard Florey</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernst_Boris_Chain" title="Ernst Boris Chain">Chain</a> in 1939, the importance of antibiotics, including antibacterials, to <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> has led to intense research into producing antibacterials at large scales. Following screening of antibacterials against a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, production of the active compounds is carried out using <a href="/wiki/Industrial_fermentation" title="Industrial fermentation">fermentation</a>, usually in strongly <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aerobic" title="wikt:aerobic">aerobic</a> conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resistance">Resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">Antibiotic resistance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2501" data-file-width="1894" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg/220px-Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg/330px-Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg/440px-Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_neutrophil_ingesting_MRSA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_micrograph" title="Scanning electron micrograph">Scanning electron micrograph</a> of a human <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophil</a> ingesting <a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">methicillin-resistant <i>Staphylococcus aureus</i></a> (MRSA)</div></div></div>
<p>The emergence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic-resistant_bacteria" title="Antibiotic-resistant bacteria">antibiotic-resistant bacteria</a> is a common phenomenon. Emergence of resistance often reflects <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> processes that take place during antibiotic therapy. The antibiotic treatment may <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">select</a> for bacterial strains with physiologically or genetically enhanced capacity to survive high doses of antibiotics. Under certain conditions, it may result in preferential growth of resistant bacteria, while growth of susceptible bacteria is inhibited by the drug.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balancing_the_drug-resistance_equation_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balancing_the_drug-resistance_equation-74">[74]</a></sup> For example, antibacterial selection for strains having previously acquired antibacterial-resistance genes was demonstrated in 1943 by the <a href="/wiki/Luria%E2%80%93Delbr%C3%BCck_experiment" title="Luria–Delbrück experiment">Luria–Delbrück experiment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mutations_of_Bacteria_from_Virus_Sensitivity_to_Virus_Resistance_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mutations_of_Bacteria_from_Virus_Sensitivity_to_Virus_Resistance-75">[75]</a></sup> Antibiotics such as penicillin and erythromycin, which used to have a high efficacy against many bacterial species and strains, have become less effective, due to the increased resistance of many bacterial strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voanews.com_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-voanews.com-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Resistance may take the form of biodegradation of pharmaceuticals, such as sulfamethazine-degrading soil bacteria introduced to sulfamethazine through medicated pig feces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
The survival of bacteria often results from an inheritable resistance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte2004_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witte2004-78">[78]</a></sup> but the growth of resistance to antibacterials also occurs through <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a>. Horizontal transfer is more likely to happen in locations of frequent antibiotic use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antibacterial resistance may impose a biological cost, thereby reducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_fitness" title="Biological fitness">fitness</a> of resistant strains, which can limit the spread of antibacterial-resistant bacteria, for example, in the absence of antibacterial compounds. Additional mutations, however, may compensate for this fitness cost and can aid the survival of these bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_biological_cost_of_mutational_antibiotic_resistance:_any_practical_conclusions?_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_biological_cost_of_mutational_antibiotic_resistance:_any_practical_conclusions?-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paleontological data show that both antibiotics and antibiotic resistance are ancient compounds and mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'Costa2011_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-D'Costa2011-81">[81]</a></sup> Useful antibiotic targets are those for which mutations negatively impact bacterial reproduction or viability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladki2013_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gladki2013-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several molecular mechanisms of antibacterial resistance exist. Intrinsic antibacterial resistance may be part of the genetic makeup of bacterial strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alekshun2007_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alekshun2007-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> For example, an antibiotic target may be absent from the bacterial <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>. Acquired resistance results from a mutation in the bacterial chromosome or the acquisition of extra-chromosomal DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alekshun2007_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alekshun2007-83">[83]</a></sup> Antibacterial-producing bacteria have evolved resistance mechanisms that have been shown to be similar to, and may have been transferred to, antibacterial-resistant strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glycopeptide_antibiotic_resistance_genes_in_glycopeptide-producing_organisms_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glycopeptide_antibiotic_resistance_genes_in_glycopeptide-producing_organisms-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Multidrug_Resistance_in_Bacteria_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Multidrug_Resistance_in_Bacteria-86">[86]</a></sup> The spread of antibacterial resistance often occurs through vertical transmission of mutations during growth and by genetic recombination of DNA by <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal genetic exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte2004_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Witte2004-78">[78]</a></sup> For instance, antibacterial resistance genes can be exchanged between different bacterial strains or species via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasmids" title="Plasmids">plasmids</a> that carry these resistance genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte2004_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Witte2004-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker2006_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker2006-87">[87]</a></sup> Plasmids that carry several different resistance genes can confer resistance to multiple antibacterials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker2006_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baker2006-87">[87]</a></sup> Cross-resistance to several antibacterials may also occur when a resistance mechanism encoded by a single gene conveys resistance to more than one antibacterial compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker2006_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baker2006-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antibacterial-resistant strains and species, sometimes referred to as "superbugs", now contribute to the emergence of diseases that were, for a while, well controlled. For example, emergent bacterial strains causing tuberculosis that are resistant to previously effective antibacterial treatments pose many therapeutic challenges. Every year, nearly half a million new cases of <a href="/wiki/Multidrug-resistant_tuberculosis" title="Multidrug-resistant tuberculosis">multidrug-resistant tuberculosis</a> (MDR-TB) are estimated to occur worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NDM-1" title="NDM-1">NDM-1</a> is a newly identified enzyme conveying bacterial resistance to a broad range of <a href="/wiki/Beta-lactam" title="Beta-lactam">beta-lactam</a> antibacterials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Are_you_ready_for_a_world_without_antibiotics?_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Are_you_ready_for_a_world_without_antibiotics?-89">[89]</a></sup> The United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Health_Protection_Agency" title="Health Protection Agency">Health Protection Agency</a> has stated that "most isolates with NDM-1 enzyme are resistant to all standard intravenous antibiotics for treatment of severe infections."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Health_Protection_Report_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Health_Protection_Report-90">[90]</a></sup> On 26 May 2016, an <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i> "<a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">superbug</a>" was identified in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> resistant to <a href="/wiki/Colistin" title="Colistin">colistin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_of_last_resort" title="Drug of last resort">"the last line of defence" antibiotic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Misuse">Misuse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Misuse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1545" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png/220px-CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png/330px-CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png/440px-CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CDC_Get_Smart_poster_healthy_adult.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This poster from the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention "Get Smart" campaign, intended for use in doctors' offices and other healthcare facilities, warns that antibiotics do not work for viral illnesses such as the common cold.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_misuse" title="Antibiotic misuse">Antibiotic misuse</a></div>
<p>Per <i>The ICU Book</i> "The first rule of antibiotics is to try not to use them, and the second rule is try not to use too many of them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marino_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marino-93">[93]</a></sup> Inappropriate antibiotic treatment and overuse of antibiotics have contributed to the emergence of antibiotic-resistant bacteria.  However, potential harm from antibiotics extends beyond selection of antimicrobial resistance and their overuse is associated with adverse effects for patients themselves, seen most clearly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critically_ill" title="Critically ill">critically ill</a> patients in <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_unit" title="Intensive care unit">Intensive care units</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-94">[94]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-prescribing" title="Self-prescribing">Self-prescribing</a> of antibiotics is an example of misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2007_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2007-95">[95]</a></sup> Many antibiotics are frequently prescribed to treat symptoms or diseases that do not respond to antibiotics or that are likely to resolve without treatment. Also, incorrect or suboptimal antibiotics are prescribed for certain bacterial infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15993671_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15993671-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2007_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2007-95">[95]</a></sup> The overuse of antibiotics, like penicillin and erythromycin, has been associated with emerging antibiotic resistance since the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voanews.com_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-voanews.com-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawkey2008_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkey2008-96">[96]</a></sup> Widespread usage of antibiotics in hospitals has also been associated with increases in bacterial strains and species that no longer respond to treatment with the most common antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawkey2008_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkey2008-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common forms of antibiotic misuse include excessive use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophylaxis" title="Prophylaxis">prophylactic</a> antibiotics in travelers and failure of medical professionals to prescribe the correct dosage of antibiotics on the basis of the patient's weight and history of prior use. Other forms of misuse include failure to take the entire prescribed course of the antibiotic, incorrect dosage and administration, or failure to rest for sufficient recovery. Inappropriate antibiotic treatment, for example, is their prescription to treat viral infections such as the <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>. One study on <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract_infection" title="Respiratory tract infection">respiratory tract infections</a> found "physicians were more likely to prescribe antibiotics to patients who appeared to expect them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17467120_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17467120-97">[97]</a></sup> Multifactorial interventions aimed at both physicians and patients can reduce inappropriate prescription of antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17509729_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17509729-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The lack of rapid point of care diagnostic tests, particularly in resource-limited settings is considered one of the drivers of antibiotic misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several organizations concerned with antimicrobial resistance are lobbying to eliminate the unnecessary use of antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2007_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2007-95">[95]</a></sup> The issues of misuse and overuse of antibiotics have been addressed by the formation of the US Interagency Task Force on Antimicrobial Resistance. This task force aims to actively address antimicrobial resistance, and is coordinated by the US <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA), and the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>, as well as other US agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pharmguide_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-pharmguide-101">[101]</a></sup> A non-governmental organization campaign group is <i>Keep Antibiotics Working</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keep_Antibiotics_Working_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keep_Antibiotics_Working-102">[102]</a></sup> In France, an "Antibiotics are not automatic" government campaign started in 2002 and led to a marked reduction of unnecessary antibiotic prescriptions, especially in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emergence of antibiotic resistance has prompted restrictions on their use in the UK in 1970 (Swann report 1969), and the European Union has banned the use of antibiotics as growth-promotional agents since 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Moreover, several organizations (including the World Health Organization, the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="U.S. Food and Drug Administration">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a>) have advocated restricting the amount of antibiotic use in food animal production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag may rely on an unreliable or less reliable medical source. (July 2016)">unreliable medical source?</span></a></i>]</sup> However, commonly there are delays in regulatory and legislative actions to limit the use of antibiotics, attributable partly to resistance against such regulation by industries using or selling antibiotics, and to the time required for research to test causal links between their use and resistance to them. Two federal bills (S.742<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USbill1_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-USbill1-106">[106]</a></sup> and H.R. 2562<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USbill2_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-USbill2-107">[107]</a></sup>) aimed at phasing out nontherapeutic use of antibiotics in US food animals were proposed, but have not passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USbill1_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-USbill1-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USbill2_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-USbill2-107">[107]</a></sup> These bills were endorsed by public health and medical organizations, including the American Holistic Nurses' Association, the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/American_Public_Health_Association" title="American Public Health Association">American Public Health Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite pledges by food companies and restaurants to reduce or eliminate meat that comes from animals treated with antibiotics, the purchase of antibiotics for use on farm animals has been increasing every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>There has been extensive use of antibiotics in animal husbandry. In the United States, the question of emergence of antibiotic-resistant bacterial strains due to <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_use_in_livestock" title="Antibiotic use in livestock">use of antibiotics in livestock</a> was raised by the US <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) in 1977. In March 2012, the United States District Court for the Southern District of New York, ruling in an action brought by the <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Defense_Council" title="Natural Resources Defense Council">Natural Resources Defense Council</a> and others, ordered the FDA to revoke approvals for the use of antibiotics in livestock, which violated FDA regulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies have shown that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_misconceptions" title="Common misconceptions">common misconceptions</a> about the effectiveness and necessity of antibiotics to treat common mild illnesses contribute to their overuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other forms of antibiotic associated harm include <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adverse_drug_reaction" title="Adverse drug reaction">drug toxicity</a> most notably kidney and liver damage, and super-infections with resistant organisms.  Antibiotics are also known to affect <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrial</a> function,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and this may contribute to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bioenergetic_failure&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bioenergetic failure (page does not exist)">bioenergetic failure</a> of <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">immune cells</a> seen in <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>  They also alter the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbiome_of_humans" title="Microbiome of humans">microbiome</a> of the gut, lungs and skin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> which may be associated with adverse effects such as <a href="/wiki/Clostridioides_difficile_infection" title="Clostridioides difficile infection">Clostridium difficile associated diarrhoea</a>.  Whilst antibiotics can clearly be lifesaving in patients with bacterial infections, their overuse, especially in patients where infections are hard to diagnose, can lead to harm via multiple mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_antibiotics" title="Timeline of antibiotics">Timeline of antibiotics</a></div>
<p>Before the early 20th century, treatments for infections were based primarily on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_medicine" title="Folk medicine">medicinal folklore</a>. Mixtures with antimicrobial properties that were used in treatments of infections were described over 2,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Considerations_for_Determining_if_a_Natural_Product_Is_an_Effective_Wound-Healing_Agent_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Considerations_for_Determining_if_a_Natural_Product_Is_an_Effective_Wound-Healing_Agent-117">[117]</a></sup> Many ancient cultures, including the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_medicine" title="Ancient Egyptian medicine">ancient Egyptians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">ancient Greeks</a>, used specially selected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">mold</a> and plant materials to treat <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Early_history_of_wound_treatment_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Early_history_of_wound_treatment-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moulds_in_ancient_and_more_recent_medicine_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moulds_in_ancient_and_more_recent_medicine-119">[119]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nubian_people" title="Nubian people">Nubian</a> mummies studied in the 1990s were found to contain significant levels of <a href="/wiki/Tetracycline" title="Tetracycline">tetracycline</a>. The beer brewed at that time was conjectured to have been the source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of antibiotics in modern medicine began with the discovery of synthetic antibiotics derived from dyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CALDERIN2007_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-CALDERIN2007-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Limbird2004_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Limbird2004-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bosch2008_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bosch2008-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodman_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-goodman-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthetic_antibiotics_derived_from_dyes">Synthetic antibiotics derived from dyes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Synthetic antibiotics derived from dyes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salvarsan-montage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="1809" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Salvarsan-montage.png/200px-Salvarsan-montage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Salvarsan-montage.png/300px-Salvarsan-montage.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Salvarsan-montage.png/400px-Salvarsan-montage.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salvarsan-montage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arsphenamine, also known as salvarsan, discovered in 1907 by Paul Ehrlich.</div></div></div>
<p>Synthetic antibiotic chemotherapy as a science and development of antibacterials began in Germany with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a> in the late 1880s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CALDERIN2007_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-CALDERIN2007-14">[14]</a></sup> Ehrlich noted certain dyes would colour human, animal, or bacterial cells, whereas others did not. He then proposed the idea that it might be possible to create chemicals that would act as a selective drug that would bind to and kill bacteria without harming the human host. After screening hundreds of dyes against various organisms, in 1907, he discovered a medicinally useful drug, the first synthetic antibacterial <a href="/wiki/Organoarsenic_chemistry" title="Organoarsenic chemistry">organoarsenic compound</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvarsan" title="Salvarsan">salvarsan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CALDERIN2007_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-CALDERIN2007-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Limbird2004_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Limbird2004-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bosch2008_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bosch2008-122">[122]</a></sup> now called arsphenamine.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3630" data-file-width="2909" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg/220px-Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg/330px-Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg/440px-Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Ehrlich_and_Sahachiro_Hata.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sahachiro_Hata" title="Sahachiro Hata">Sahachiro Hata</a></div></div></div>
