<p>This heralded the era of antibacterial treatment that was begun with the discovery of a series of arsenic-derived synthetic antibiotics by both <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Bertheim" title="Alfred Bertheim">Alfred Bertheim</a> and Ehrlich in 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodman_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-goodman-124">[124]</a></sup> Ehrlich and Bertheim had experimented with various chemicals derived from dyes to treat <a href="/wiki/Trypanosomiasis" title="Trypanosomiasis">trypanosomiasis</a> in mice and <a href="/wiki/Spirochaeta" title="Spirochaeta">spirochaeta</a> infection in rabbits. While their early compounds were too toxic, Ehrlich and <a href="/wiki/Sahachiro_Hata" title="Sahachiro Hata">Sahachiro Hata</a>, a Japanese bacteriologist working with Erlich in the quest for a drug to treat <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, achieved success with the 606th compound in their series of experiments. In 1910, Ehrlich and Hata announced their discovery, which they called drug "606", at the Congress for Internal Medicine at <a href="/wiki/Wiesbaden" title="Wiesbaden">Wiesbaden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmvh.org_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmvh.org-125">[125]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hoechst_AG" title="Hoechst AG">Hoechst</a> company began to market the compound toward the end of 1910 under the name Salvarsan, now known as <a href="/wiki/Arsphenamine" title="Arsphenamine">arsphenamine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmvh.org_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-jmvh.org-125">[125]</a></sup> The drug was used to treat syphilis in the first half of the 20th century. In 1908, Ehrlich received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> for his contributions to <a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobel_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobel-126">[126]</a></sup> Hata was nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 1911 and for the Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine in 1912 and 1913.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(medicine)" title="Sulfonamide (medicine)">sulfonamide</a> and the first <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/systemic" title="wikt:systemic">systemically</a> active antibacterial drug, <a href="/wiki/Prontosil" title="Prontosil">Prontosil</a>, was developed by a research team led by <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Domagk" title="Gerhard Domagk">Gerhard Domagk</a> in 1932 or 1933 at the <a href="/wiki/Bayer" title="Bayer">Bayer</a> Laboratories of the <a href="/wiki/IG_Farben" title="IG Farben">IG Farben</a> conglomerate in Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodman_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-goodman-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bosch2008_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bosch2008-122">[122]</a></sup> for which Domagk received the 1939 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Sulfanilamide, the active drug of Prontosil, was not patentable as it had already been in use in the dye industry for some years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-128">[128]</a></sup> Prontosil had a relatively broad effect against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-positive" title="Gram-positive">Gram-positive</a> <a href="/wiki/Coccus" title="Coccus">cocci</a>, but not against <a href="/wiki/Enterobacteriaceae" title="Enterobacteriaceae">enterobacteria</a>. Research was stimulated apace by its success. The discovery and development of this sulfonamide <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drug</a> opened the era of antibacterials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penicillin_and_other_natural_antibiotics">Penicillin and other natural antibiotics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Penicillin and other natural antibiotics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_penicillin" title="History of penicillin">History of penicillin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Penicillin_core.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Penicillin_core.svg/170px-Penicillin_core.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Penicillin_core.svg/255px-Penicillin_core.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Penicillin_core.svg/340px-Penicillin_core.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Penicillin_core.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">Penicillin</a>, discovered by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Fleming" title="Alexander Fleming">Alexander Fleming</a> in 1928</div></div></div>
<p>Observations about the growth of some microorganisms inhibiting the growth of other microorganisms have been reported since the late 19th century. These observations of antibiosis between microorganisms led to the discovery of natural antibacterials. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> observed, "if we could intervene in the antagonism observed between some bacteria, it would offer perhaps the greatest hopes for therapeutics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingston2008_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kingston2008-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1874, physician Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Roberts_(physician)" title="William Roberts (physician)">William Roberts</a> noted that cultures of the mould <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_glaucum" title="Penicillium glaucum">Penicillium glaucum</a></i> that is used in the making of some types of <a href="/wiki/Blue_cheese" title="Blue cheese">blue cheese</a> did not display bacterial contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In 1876, physicist <a href="/wiki/John_Tyndall" title="John Tyndall">John Tyndall</a> also contributed to this field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1895 <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Tiberio" title="Vincenzo Tiberio">Vincenzo Tiberio</a>, Italian physician, published a paper on the antibacterial power of some extracts of mold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1897, doctoral student <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Duchesne" title="Ernest Duchesne">Ernest Duchesne</a> submitted a dissertation, "<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Contribution à l'étude de la concurrence vitale chez les micro-organismes: antagonisme entre les moisissures et les microbes</i></span>" (Contribution to the study of vital competition in micro-organisms: antagonism between moulds and microbes),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> the first known scholarly work to consider the therapeutic capabilities of moulds resulting from their anti-microbial activity. In his thesis, Duchesne proposed that bacteria and moulds engage in a perpetual battle for survival. Duchesne observed that <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i> was eliminated by <i>Penicillium glaucum</i> when they were both grown in the same culture. He also observed that when he <a href="/wiki/Inoculation" title="Inoculation">inoculated</a> laboratory animals with lethal doses of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a> bacilli together with <i>Penicillium glaucum</i>, the animals did not contract typhoid. Unfortunately Duchesne's army service after getting his degree prevented him from doing any further research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Academic_Press_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Academic_Press-137">[137]</a></sup> Duchesne died of <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, a disease now treated by antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Academic_Press_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Academic_Press-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Fleming.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="385" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Alexander_Fleming.jpg/220px-Alexander_Fleming.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Alexander_Fleming.jpg/330px-Alexander_Fleming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Alexander_Fleming.jpg/440px-Alexander_Fleming.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Fleming.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander Fleming was awarded a Nobel prize for his role in the discovery of penicillin</div></div></div><p>In 1928, Sir <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Fleming" title="Alexander Fleming">Alexander Fleming</a> postulated the existence of <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>, a molecule produced by certain moulds that kills or stops the growth of certain kinds of bacteria. Fleming was working on a culture of <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">disease-causing</a> bacteria when he noticed the <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> of a green mold, <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_rubens" title="Penicillium rubens">Penicillium rubens</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32973216_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32973216-138">[138]</a></sup> in one of his <a href="/wiki/Agar_plate" title="Agar plate">culture plates</a>. He observed that the presence of the mould killed or prevented the growth of the bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Fleming postulated that the mould must secrete an antibacterial substance, which he named penicillin in 1928. Fleming believed that its antibacterial properties could be exploited for chemotherapy. He initially characterised some of its biological properties, and attempted to use a crude preparation to treat some infections, but he was unable to pursue its further development without the aid of trained chemists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming1929_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming1929-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sykes2001_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sykes2001-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ernst_Chain" title="Ernst Chain">Ernst Chain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Florey" title="Howard Florey">Howard Florey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Abraham" title="Edward Abraham">Edward Abraham</a> succeeded in purifying the first penicillin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penicillin_G" title="Penicillin G">penicillin G</a>, in 1942, but it did not become widely available outside the Allied military before 1945. Later, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Heatley" title="Norman Heatley">Norman Heatley</a> developed the back extraction technique for efficiently purifying penicillin in bulk. The chemical structure of penicillin was first proposed by Abraham in 1942<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> and then later confirmed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorothy_Crowfoot_Hodgkin" title="Dorothy Crowfoot Hodgkin">Dorothy Crowfoot Hodgkin</a> in 1945. Purified penicillin displayed potent antibacterial activity against a wide range of bacteria and had low toxicity in humans. Furthermore, its activity was not inhibited by biological constituents such as pus, unlike the synthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfonamides" title="Sulfonamides">sulfonamides</a>. (see below) The development of penicillin led to renewed interest in the search for antibiotic compounds with similar efficacy and safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Use_of_Micro-organisms_for_therapeutic_purposes_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Use_of_Micro-organisms_for_therapeutic_purposes-143">[143]</a></sup> For their successful development of penicillin, which Fleming had accidentally discovered but could not develop himself, as a therapeutic drug, Chain and Florey shared the 1945 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> with Fleming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Florey credited <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Dubos" title="René Dubos">René Dubos</a> with pioneering the approach of deliberately and systematically searching for antibacterial compounds, which had led to the discovery of gramicidin and had revived Florey's research in penicillin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epps2006_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epps2006-145">[145]</a></sup> In 1939, coinciding with the start of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Dubos had reported the discovery of the first naturally derived antibiotic, <a href="/wiki/Tyrothricin" title="Tyrothricin">tyrothricin</a>, a compound of 20% <a href="/wiki/Gramicidin" title="Gramicidin">gramicidin</a> and 80% <a href="/wiki/Tyrocidine" title="Tyrocidine">tyrocidine</a>, from <i>Bacillus brevis</i>. It was one of the first commercially manufactured antibiotics and was very effective in treating wounds and ulcers during World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epps2006_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Epps2006-145">[145]</a></sup> Gramicidin, however, could not be used systemically because of toxicity. Tyrocidine also proved too toxic for systemic usage. Research results obtained during that period were not shared between the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allied_powers_of_World_War_II" title="Allied powers of World War II">Allied powers</a> during World War II and limited access during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_20th_century">Late 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Late 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the mid-20th century, the number of new antibiotic substances introduced for medical use increased significantly. From 1935 to 1968, 12 new classes were launched. However, after this, the number of new classes dropped markedly, with only two new classes introduced between 1969 and 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antibiotic_pipeline">Antibiotic pipeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Antibiotic pipeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Both the WHO and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_Disease_Society_of_America" title="Infectious Disease Society of America">Infectious Disease Society of America</a> report that the weak antibiotic pipeline does not match bacteria's increasing ability to develop resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_-_analysis_of_pipeline_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_-_analysis_of_pipeline-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The Infectious Disease Society of America report noted that the number of new antibiotics approved for marketing per year had been declining and identified seven antibiotics against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-negative_bacilli" title="Gram-negative bacilli">Gram-negative bacilli</a> currently in <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research#Phase_II" title="Phases of clinical research">phase 2</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research#Phase_III" title="Phases of clinical research">phase 3</a> clinical trials. However, these drugs did not address the entire spectrum of resistance of Gram-negative bacilli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2013_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2013-151">[151]</a></sup> According to the WHO fifty one new therapeutic entities - antibiotics (including combinations), are in phase 1-3 clinical trials as of May 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_-_analysis_of_pipeline_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_-_analysis_of_pipeline-148">[148]</a></sup> Antibiotics targeting multidrug-resistant Gram-positive pathogens remains a high priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_-_analysis_of_pipeline_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_-_analysis_of_pipeline-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few antibiotics have received marketing authorization in the last seven years. The cephalosporin ceftaroline and the lipoglycopeptides oritavancin and telavancin for the treatment of acute bacterial skin and skin structure infection and community-acquired bacterial pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernandes,_Martens_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes,_Martens-153">[153]</a></sup> The lipoglycopeptide dalbavancin and the oxazolidinone tedizolid has also been approved for use for the treatment of acute bacterial skin and skin structure infection. The first in a new class of narrow spectrum <a href="/wiki/Macrocycle" title="Macrocycle">macrocyclic</a> antibiotics, fidaxomicin, has been approved for the treatment of <i>C. difficile</i> colitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernandes,_Martens_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes,_Martens-153">[153]</a></sup> New cephalosporin-lactamase inhibitor combinations also approved include ceftazidime-avibactam and ceftolozane-avibactam for complicated urinary tract infection and intra-abdominal infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernandes,_Martens_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandes,_Martens-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceftolozane" title="Ceftolozane">Ceftolozane</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tazobactam" title="Tazobactam">tazobactam</a> (CXA-201; CXA-101/tazobactam): <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antipseudomonal" title="Antipseudomonal">Antipseudomonal</a> <a href="/wiki/Cephalosporin" title="Cephalosporin">cephalosporin</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-lactamase" title="Β-lactamase">β-lactamase</a> inhibitor combination (cell wall synthesis inhibitor). FDA approved on 19 December 2014.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ceftazidime" title="Ceftazidime">Ceftazidime</a>/<a href="/wiki/Avibactam" title="Avibactam">avibactam</a> (ceftazidime/NXL104): antipseudomonal cephalosporin/β-lactamase inhibitor combination (cell wall synthesis inhibitor).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 25 February 2015.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceftaroline" title="Ceftaroline">Ceftaroline</a>/avibactam (CPT-avibactam; ceftaroline/NXL104): Anti-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRSA" title="MRSA">MRSA</a> cephalosporin/ β-lactamase inhibitor combination (cell wall synthesis inhibitor).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cefiderocol" title="Cefiderocol">Cefiderocol</a>: <a href="/wiki/Cephalosporin" title="Cephalosporin">cephalosporin</a> siderophore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 14 November 2019.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Imipenem" title="Imipenem">Imipenem</a>/relebactam: <a href="/wiki/Carbapenem" title="Carbapenem">carbapenem</a>/ β-lactamase inhibitor combination (cell wall synthesis inhibitor).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 16 July 2019.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Meropenem/vaborbactam" title="Meropenem/vaborbactam">Meropenem/vaborbactam</a>: <a href="/wiki/Carbapenem" title="Carbapenem">carbapenem</a>/ β-lactamase inhibitor combination (cell wall synthesis inhibitor).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 29 August 2017.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Delafloxacin" title="Delafloxacin">Delafloxacin</a>: <a href="/wiki/Quinolone" title="Quinolone">quinolone</a> (inhibitor of DNA synthesis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 19 June 2017.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plazomicin" title="Plazomicin">Plazomicin</a> (ACHN-490): semi-synthetic <a href="/wiki/Aminoglycoside" title="Aminoglycoside">aminoglycoside</a> derivative (<a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis_inhibitor" title="Protein synthesis inhibitor">protein synthesis inhibitor</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-5"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved 25 June 2018.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eravacycline" title="Eravacycline">Eravacycline</a> (TP-434): synthetic <a href="/wiki/Tetracycline" title="Tetracycline">tetracycline</a> derivative (protein synthesis inhibitor targeting bacterial ribosomes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-6"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 27 August 2018.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Omadacycline" title="Omadacycline">Omadacycline</a>: semi-synthetic <a href="/wiki/Tetracycline" title="Tetracycline">tetracycline</a> derivative (protein synthesis inhibitor targeting bacterial ribosomes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-7"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup> FDA approved on 2 October 2018.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lefamulin" title="Lefamulin">Lefamulin</a>: pleuromutilin antibiotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32152527_154-8"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32152527-154">[154]</a></sup>  FDA approved on 19 August 2019.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brilacidin" title="Brilacidin">Brilacidin</a> (PMX-30063): peptide defense protein mimetic (cell membrane disruption). In phase 2.</li></ul></div>
<p>Possible improvements include clarification of clinical trial regulations by FDA. Furthermore, appropriate economic incentives could persuade pharmaceutical companies to invest in this endeavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2013_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2013-151">[151]</a></sup> In the US, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_Development_to_Advance_Patient_Treatment" title="Antibiotic Development to Advance Patient Treatment">Antibiotic Development to Advance Patient Treatment</a> (ADAPT) Act was introduced with the aim of fast tracking the drug development of antibiotics to combat the growing threat of 'superbugs'. Under this Act, FDA can approve antibiotics and antifungals treating life-threatening infections based on smaller clinical trials. The <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a> will monitor the use of antibiotics and the emerging resistance, and publish the data. The FDA antibiotics labeling process, 'Susceptibility Test Interpretive Criteria for Microbial Organisms' or 'breakpoints', will provide accurate data to healthcare professionals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> According to Allan Coukell, senior director for health programs at The Pew Charitable Trusts, "By allowing drug developers to rely on smaller datasets, and clarifying FDA's authority to tolerate a higher level of uncertainty for these drugs when making a risk/benefit calculation, ADAPT would make the clinical trials more feasible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Replenishing_the_antibiotic_pipeline_and_developing_other_new_therapies">Replenishing the antibiotic pipeline and developing other new therapies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Replenishing the antibiotic pipeline and developing other new therapies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Because antibiotic-resistant bacterial strains continue to emerge and spread, there is a constant need to develop new antibacterial treatments.  Current strategies include traditional chemistry-based approaches such as <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural product</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Drug_discovery" title="Drug discovery">drug discovery</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_Products_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_Products-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32529587_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32529587-158">[158]</a></sup> newer chemistry-based approaches such as <a href="/wiki/Drug_design" title="Drug design">drug design</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32199982_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32199982-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30360704_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30360704-160">[160]</a></sup> traditional biology-based approaches such as <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_therapy" title="Immunoglobulin therapy">immunoglobulin therapy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup> and experimental biology-based approaches such as <a href="/wiki/Phage_therapy" title="Phage therapy">phage therapy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26795692_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26795692-164">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fecal_microbiota_transplant" title="Fecal microbiota transplant">fecal microbiota transplants</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antisense_RNA" title="Antisense RNA">antisense RNA</a>-based treatments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR-Cas9</a>-based treatments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32575913_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32575913-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_product-based_antibiotic_discovery">Natural product-based antibiotic discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Natural product-based antibiotic discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bioprospecting" title="Bioprospecting">Bioprospecting</a></div>

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Agelas_tubulata_cropped.jpg/262px-Agelas_tubulata_cropped.jpg 2x" width="131"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Bacteria, fungi, plants, animals and other organisms are being screened in the search for new antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32529587_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32529587-158">[158]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Most of the antibiotics in current use are <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a> or natural product derivatives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32529587_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32529587-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31733401_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31733401-167">[167]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungal" title="Fungal">fungal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_Products_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_Products-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23978412_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23978412-170">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny,_Furey,_Lucey_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny,_Furey,_Lucey-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monte_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monte-174">[174]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_Products_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_Products-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27373625_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27373625-175">[175]</a></sup> extracts are being screened in the search for new antibiotics.  Organisms may be selected for testing based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological" title="Ecological">ecological</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnomedical" title="Ethnomedical">ethnomedical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genomic" title="Genomic">genomic</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical" title="Historical">historical</a> rationales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32529587_158-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32529587-158">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Medicinal_plants" title="Medicinal plants">Medicinal plants</a>, for example, are screened on the basis that they are used by traditional healers to prevent or cure infection and may therefore contain antibacterial compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cowen_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-cowen-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plants_as_sources_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plants_as_sources-177">[177]</a></sup>  Also, soil bacteria are screened on the basis that, historically, they have been a very rich source of antibiotics (with 70 to 80% of antibiotics in current use derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinomycetes" title="Actinomycetes">actinomycetes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32529587_158-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32529587-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27890726_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27890726-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to screening natural products for direct antibacterial activity, they are sometimes screened for the ability to suppress <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_tolerance" title="Antibiotic tolerance">antibiotic tolerance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plants_as_sources_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plants_as_sources-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21562562_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21562562-179">[179]</a></sup> For example, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_metabolites" title="Secondary metabolites">secondary metabolites</a> inhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_efflux" title="Drug efflux">drug efflux</a> pumps, thereby increasing the concentration of antibiotic able to reach its cellular target and decreasing bacterial resistance to the antibiotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plants_as_sources_177-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plants_as_sources-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Efflux_pump_inhibitors_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Efflux_pump_inhibitors-180">[180]</a></sup>  Natural products known to inhibit bacterial efflux pumps include the <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloid</a> <a href="/wiki/Lysergol" title="Lysergol">lysergol</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> <a href="/wiki/Capsanthin" title="Capsanthin">capsanthin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capsorubin" title="Capsorubin">capsorubin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20645919_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20645919-182">[182]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> <a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">rotenone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrysin" title="Chrysin">chrysin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20645919_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20645919-182">[182]</a></sup>  Other natural products, this time <a href="/wiki/Primary_metabolite" title="Primary metabolite">primary metabolites</a> rather than secondary metabolites, have been shown to eradicate antibiotic tolerance.  For example, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannitol" title="Mannitol">mannitol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> reduce antibiotic tolerance in <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i>, rendering them more susceptible to killing by <a href="/wiki/Aminoglycoside" title="Aminoglycoside">aminoglycoside</a> antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21562562_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21562562-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural products may be screened for the ability to suppress bacterial <a href="/wiki/Virulence_factor" title="Virulence factor">virulence factors</a> too.  Virulence factors are molecules, cellular structures and regulatory systems that enable bacteria to evade the body's immune defenses (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Urease" title="Urease">urease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staphyloxanthin" title="Staphyloxanthin">staphyloxanthin</a>), move towards, attach to, and/or invade human cells (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_IV_pili" title="Type IV pili">type IV pili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_adhesin" title="Bacterial adhesin">adhesins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internalin" title="Internalin">internalins</a>), coordinate the activation of virulence genes (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Quorum_sensing" title="Quorum sensing">quorum sensing</a>), and cause disease (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Exotoxin" title="Exotoxin">exotoxins</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monte_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monte-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21514796_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21514796-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31410034_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31410034-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>  Examples of natural products with antivirulence activity include the flavonoid <a href="/wiki/Epigallocatechin_gallate" title="Epigallocatechin gallate">epigallocatechin gallate</a> (which inhibits <a href="/wiki/Listeriolysin_O" title="Listeriolysin O">listeriolysin O</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21514796_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21514796-183">[183]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Quinone" title="Quinone">quinone</a> tetrangomycin (which inhibits staphyloxanthin),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31410034_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31410034-184">[184]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Sesquiterpene" title="Sesquiterpene">sesquiterpene</a> zerumbone (which inhibits <i><a href="/wiki/Acinetobacter_baumannii" title="Acinetobacter baumannii">Acinetobacter baumannii</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria#Movement" title="Bacteria">motility</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32463353_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32463353-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunoglobulin_therapy">Immunoglobulin therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Immunoglobulin therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibody_therapy" title="Monoclonal antibody therapy">Monoclonal antibody therapy</a></div>
<p>Antibodies (<a href="/wiki/Anti-tetanus_immunoglobulin" title="Anti-tetanus immunoglobulin">anti-tetanus immunoglobulin</a>) have been used in the treatment and prevention  of <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a> since the 1910s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> and this approach continues to be a useful way of controlling bacterial diseases.  The <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibody" title="Monoclonal antibody">monoclonal antibody</a> <a href="/wiki/Bezlotoxumab" title="Bezlotoxumab">bezlotoxumab</a>, for example, has been approved by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">US FDA</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Medicines_Agency" title="European Medicines Agency">EMA</a> for recurrent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_infection" title="Clostridium difficile infection"><i>Clostridium difficile</i> infection</a>, and other monoclonal antibodies are in development (e.g. AR-301 for the adjunctive treatment of <i>S. aureus</i> <a href="/wiki/Ventilator-associated_pneumonia" title="Ventilator-associated pneumonia">ventilator-associated pneumonia</a>).  Antibody treatments act by binding to and neutralizing bacterial exotoxins and other virulence factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-5"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phage_therapy">Phage therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Phage therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phage_therapy" title="Phage therapy">Phage therapy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2013" data-file-width="2316" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg/160px-Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg/240px-Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg/320px-Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phage_injecting_its_genome_into_bacteria.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phage injecting its genome into a bacterium.  Viral replication and bacterial cell lysis will ensue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sulakvelidze_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sulakvelidze-188">[188]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phage_therapy" title="Phage therapy">Phage therapy</a> is under investigation as a method of treating antibiotic-resistant strains of bacteria. Phage therapy involves infecting bacterial pathogens with <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">Bacteriophages</a> and their host ranges are extremely specific for certain bacteria, thus, unlike antibiotics, they do not disturb the host organism's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestinal_microbiota" title="Intestinal microbiota">intestinal microbiota</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock-189">[189]</a></sup> Bacteriophages, also known simply as phages, infect and kill bacteria primarily during lytic cycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sulakvelidze_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sulakvelidze-188">[188]</a></sup> Phages insert their DNA into the bacterium, where it is transcribed and used to make new phages, after which the cell will lyse, releasing new phage that are able to infect and destroy further bacteria of the same strain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sulakvelidze_188-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sulakvelidze-188">[188]</a></sup> The high specificity of phage protects <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">"good"</a> bacteria from destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some disadvantages to the use of bacteriophages also exist, however. Bacteriophages may harbour virulence factors or toxic genes in their genomes and, prior to use, it may be prudent to identify genes with similarity to known virulence factors or toxins by genomic sequencing. In addition, the oral and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous" title="Intravenous">IV</a> administration of phages for the eradication of bacterial infections poses a much higher safety risk than topical application. Also, there is the additional concern of uncertain immune responses to these large antigenic cocktails.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There are considerable <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_therapeutic_goods" title="Regulation of therapeutic goods">regulatory</a> hurdles that must be cleared for such therapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock_189-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock-189">[189]</a></sup> Despite numerous challenges, the use of bacteriophages as a replacement for antimicrobial agents against MDR pathogens that no longer respond to conventional antibiotics, remains an attractive option.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock_189-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gill,_Franco,_Hancock-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fecal_microbiota_transplants">Fecal microbiota transplants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fecal microbiota transplants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fecal_microbiota_transplant" title="Fecal microbiota transplant">Fecal microbiota transplant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E_coli_at_10000x,_original.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1889" data-file-width="2598" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg/160px-E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg/240px-E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg/320px-E_coli_at_10000x%2C_original.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E_coli_at_10000x,_original.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fecal microbiota transplants are an experimental treatment for <i>C. difficile</i> infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-6"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Fecal microbiota transplants involve transferring the full <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestinal_microbiota" title="Intestinal microbiota">intestinal microbiota</a> from a healthy human donor (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">stool</a>) to patients with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_infection" title="Clostridium difficile infection"><i>C. difficile</i> infection</a>.  Although this procedure has not been officially approved by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">US FDA</a>, its use is permitted under some conditions in patients with antibiotic-resistant <i>C. difficile</i> infection.  Cure rates are around 90%, and work is underway to develop stool <a href="/wiki/Biobank" title="Biobank">banks</a>, standardized products, and methods of <a href="/wiki/Oral_administration" title="Oral administration">oral delivery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-7"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antisense_RNA-based_treatments">Antisense RNA-based treatments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Antisense RNA-based treatments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Antisense_RNA" title="Antisense RNA">Antisense RNA</a></div>
<p>Antisense RNA-based treatment (also known as gene silencing therapy) involves (a) identifying bacterial <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> that encode essential <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> (e.g. the <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i> genes <i>acpP</i>, <i>lpxC</i>, and <i>rpsJ</i>), (b) synthesizing single stranded <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> that is complementary to the <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">mRNA</a> encoding these essential proteins, and (c) delivering the single stranded RNA to the infection site using cell-penetrating peptides or <a href="/wiki/Liposome" title="Liposome">liposomes</a>.  The antisense RNA then <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_hybridization" title="Nucleic acid hybridization">hybridizes</a> with the bacterial mRNA and blocks its <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a> into the essential protein.  Antisense RNA-based treatment has been shown to be effective in <i>in vivo</i> models of <i>P. aeruginosa</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lung_infection" title="Lung infection">pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-8"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to silencing essential bacterial genes, antisense RNA can be used to silence bacterial genes responsible for antibiotic resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-9"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-5"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup>  For example, antisense RNA has been developed that silences the <i>S. aureus</i> <i><a href="/wiki/MecA" title="MecA">mecA</a></i> gene (the gene that encodes modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penicillin-binding_protein" title="Penicillin-binding protein">penicillin-binding protein</a> 2a and renders <i>S. aureus</i> strains <a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">methicillin-resistant</a>).  Antisense RNA targeting <i>mecA</i> mRNA has been shown to restore the susceptibility of methicillin-resistant staphylococci to <a href="/wiki/Oxacillin" title="Oxacillin">oxacillin</a> in both <i>in vitro</i> and <i>in vivo</i> studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-6"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CRISPR-Cas9-based_treatments">CRISPR-Cas9-based treatments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: CRISPR-Cas9-based treatments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 2000s, a system was discovered that enables bacteria to defend themselves against invading viruses.  The system, known as CRISPR-Cas9, consists of (a) an enzyme that destroys DNA (the <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nuclease</a> <a href="/wiki/Cas9" title="Cas9">Cas9</a>) and (b) the DNA sequences of previously encountered viral invaders (<a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a>).  These viral DNA sequences enable the nuclease to target foreign (viral) rather than self (bacterial) DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29358495_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29358495-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the function of CRISPR-Cas9 in nature is to protect bacteria, the DNA sequences in the CRISPR component of the system can be modified so that the Cas9 nuclease targets bacterial <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">resistance</a> genes or bacterial <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> genes instead of viral genes.  The modified CRISPR-Cas9 system can then be administered to bacterial pathogens using plasmids or bacteriophages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31295426_161-10"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31295426-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-7"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup>  This approach has successfully been used to <a href="/wiki/Gene_silencing" title="Gene silencing">silence</a> antibiotic resistance and reduce the virulence of <a href="/wiki/Shigatoxigenic_and_verotoxigenic_Escherichia_coli" title="Shigatoxigenic and verotoxigenic Escherichia coli">enterohemorrhagic <i>E. coli</i></a> in an <i>in vivo</i> model of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30683453_162-8"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30683453-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_the_selection_pressure_for_antibiotic_resistance">Reducing the selection pressure for antibiotic resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Reducing the selection pressure for antibiotic resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities%2C_OWID.svg/220px-Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities%2C_OWID.svg/330px-Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities%2C_OWID.svg/440px-Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Share_of_population_using_safely_managed_sanitation_facilities,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of population using safely managed sanitation facilities in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In addition to developing new antibacterial treatments, it is important to reduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selection_pressure" title="Selection pressure">selection pressure</a> for the emergence and spread of <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>.  Strategies to accomplish this include well-established infection control measures such as infrastructure improvement (e.g. less crowded housing),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30545014_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30545014-195">[195]</a></sup> better sanitation (e.g. safe drinking water and food)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21453872_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21453872-197">[197]</a></sup> and vaccine development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26795692_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26795692-164">[164]</a></sup> other approaches such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_stewardship" title="Antibiotic stewardship">antibiotic stewardship</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31836329_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31836329-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31585470_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31585470-199">[199]</a></sup> and experimental approaches such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Prebiotic_(nutrition)" title="Prebiotic (nutrition)">prebiotics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Probiotics" title="Probiotics">probiotics</a> to prevent infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32800382_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32800382-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31083597_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31083597-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Antibiotic cycling, where antibiotics are alternated by clinicians to treat microbial diseases, is proposed, but recent studies revealed such strategies are ineffective against antibiotic resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccines">Vaccines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antibiotic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Vaccines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">Vaccines</a> rely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune" title="Immune">immune</a> modulation or augmentation. Vaccination either excites or reinforces the immune competence of a host to ward off infection, leading to the activation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrophages" title="Macrophages">macrophages</a>, the production of <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>, and other classic immune reactions. Antibacterial vaccines have been responsible for a drastic reduction in global bacterial diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Vaccines made from attenuated whole cells or lysates have been replaced largely by less reactogenic, cell-free vaccines consisting of purified components, including capsular polysaccharides and their conjugates, to protein carriers, as well as inactivated toxins (toxoids) and proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFFosterRaoult1974">Foster W, Raoult A (December 1974). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2157443" rel="nofollow">"Early descriptions of antibiosis"</a>. <i>The Journal of the Royal College of General Practitioners</i>. <b>24</b> (149): 889–94. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2157443" rel="nofollow">2157443</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/4618289" rel="nofollow">4618289</a>. <q>Both Roberts and Tyndall indicated that the Penicillium moulds had some property or had an activity which inhibited bacterial growth.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Journal+of+the+Royal+College+of+General+Practitioners&amp;rft.atitle=Early+descriptions+of+antibiosis&amp;rft.volume=24&amp;rft.issue=149&amp;rft.pages=889-94&amp;rft.date=1974-12&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2157443%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F4618289&amp;rft.aulast=Foster&amp;rft.aufirst=W&amp;rft.au=Raoult%2C+A&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2157443&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAntibiotic"></span></span>
